   <div class="mw-indicators">
   </div>
   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    List of fatal bear attacks in North America
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America_by_decade&amp;redirect=no" title="List of fatal bear attacks in North America by decade">
       List of fatal bear attacks in North America by decade
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beardanger.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="943" data-file-width="1259" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Beardanger.JPG/220px-Beardanger.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Beardanger.JPG/330px-Beardanger.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Beardanger.JPG/440px-Beardanger.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beardanger.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Bear danger area closure sign of the type used at
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denali_National_Park" title="Denali National Park">
         Denali National Park
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear,_Churchill,_Manitoba,_Canada..jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2452" data-file-width="2472" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Polar_Bear%2C_Churchill%2C_Manitoba%2C_Canada..jpg/150px-Polar_Bear%2C_Churchill%2C_Manitoba%2C_Canada..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Polar_Bear%2C_Churchill%2C_Manitoba%2C_Canada..jpg/225px-Polar_Bear%2C_Churchill%2C_Manitoba%2C_Canada..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Polar_Bear%2C_Churchill%2C_Manitoba%2C_Canada..jpg/300px-Polar_Bear%2C_Churchill%2C_Manitoba%2C_Canada..jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear,_Churchill,_Manitoba,_Canada..jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A polar bear in
        <a href="/wiki/Churchill,_Manitoba" title="Churchill, Manitoba">
         Churchill, Manitoba
        </a>
        . Fatal polar bear attacks occurred in Churchill in 1968 and 1983.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Fatal
      <a href="/wiki/Bear_attack" title="Bear attack">
       bear attacks
      </a>
      in North America have occurred in a variety of settings. There have been several in the bears'
      <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">
       wilderness
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">
       habitats
      </a>
      involving hikers, hunters, and campers.
      <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">
       Brown bear
      </a>
      incidents have occurred in their native range spanning
      <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">
       Alaska
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Northern_Canada" title="Northern Canada">
       Northern
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada" title="Western Canada">
       Western Canada
      </a>
      , and portions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">
       Rocky Mountains
      </a>
      in the United States. The locations of
      <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">
       black bear
      </a>
      wilderness fatal attacks reflect their wider range: all Canadian Provinces except the
      <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Canada" title="Atlantic Canada">
       Atlantic Provinces
      </a>
      and several major mountainous areas in the United States.
     </p>
     <p>
      Bears held
      <a href="/wiki/Captivity_(animal)" title="Captivity (animal)">
       captive
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Animal_training" title="Animal training">
       animal trainers
      </a>
      , in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoos" title="Zoos">
       zoos
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivals" title="Carnivals">
       carnivals
      </a>
      , or kept as pets have been responsible for several attacks. There have also been unusual cases in which a person entered a bear's cage and was then mauled.
     </p>
     <p>
      Bear attacks are extremely rare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-outsideMarch2001_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-outsideMarch2001-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msnbc162009_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-msnbc162009-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt4July1995_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt4July1995-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Attacks are for
      <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">
       predatory
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">
       territorial
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Bear_attack#Protecting_young" title="Bear attack">
       protective
      </a>
      reasons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stpaug21978_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stpaug21978-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most wilderness attacks have occurred when there was only one to two persons in the vicinity, and no attacks have ever been recorded against groups of six or more.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journal_wildlife_2011_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-journal_wildlife_2011-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In this list, three species of
      <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">
       bear
      </a>
      are recognized: the
      <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">
       brown bear
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Ursus arctos
      </i>
      ), the
      <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">
       American black bear
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Ursus americanus
      </i>
      ), and the
      <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">
       polar bear
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Ursus maritimus
      </i>
      ).
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">
       2010s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: 2010s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Black_bear">
       Black bear
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Black bear">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Name, age, gender
       </th>
       <th style="width:100px;">
        Date
       </th>
       <th>
        Type
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Location
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Description
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Darsh Patel, 22, male
       </td>
       <td>
        September 21, 2014
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        near
        <a href="/wiki/West_Milford,_New_Jersey" title="West Milford, New Jersey">
         West Milford, New Jersey
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Patel was about to begin hiking with four friends in
        <a href="/wiki/Apshawa_Preserve" title="Apshawa Preserve">
         Apshawa Preserve
        </a>
        when they met a man and a woman at the entrance who told them there was a bear nearby and advised them to turn around.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyd_11252014_7-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nyd_11252014-7">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          7
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        They continued on, found the bear, and Patel and another hiker took photos. They turned and began walking away, but the bear followed them. The hikers ran in different directions, and found that Patel was missing when they regrouped. Authorities found Patel's body after searching for two hours. A black bear found in the vicinity was killed.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_nj2014_8-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nyt_nj2014-8">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          8
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        According to the State Department of Environmental Protection, the last fatal bear attack in New Jersey was in 1852.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_nj2014_8-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-nyt_nj2014-8">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          8
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Lorna Weafer, 36, female
       </td>
       <td>
        May 7, 2014
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        near
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_McMurray,_Alberta" title="Fort McMurray, Alberta">
         Fort McMurray, Alberta
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Weafer, a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suncor" title="Suncor">
         Suncor
        </a>
        worker was attacked at the remote North Steepbank
        <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_oil_sands" title="Athabasca oil sands">
         oil sands
        </a>
        mine site while walking back to work after a trip to the
        <a href="/wiki/Public_toilet" title="Public toilet">
         washroom
        </a>
        . Efforts by co-workers to scare off the bear were unsuccessful. The
        <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">
         Royal Canadian Mounted Police
        </a>
        shot and killed the bear upon arrival. A preliminary investigation determined that the attack was predatory.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc_weafer1_9-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cbc_weafer1-9">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          9
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Robert Weaver, 64, male
       </td>
       <td>
        June 6, 2013
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        George Lake near
        <a href="/wiki/Delta_Junction,_Alaska" title="Delta Junction, Alaska">
         Delta Junction, Alaska
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Weaver was attacked by a black bear while walking back to his cabin on George Lake, according to his wife, who was able to flee inside the cabin and was uninjured. A 230lb (104.3kg) adult male black bear on the scene was killed by troopers and found to have some of Weaver's remains in his stomach.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsminer11june2013_10-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-newsminer11june2013-10">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          10
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Lana Hollingsworth, 61, female
       </td>
       <td>
        July 25, 2011
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Pinetop-Lakeside,_Arizona" title="Pinetop-Lakeside, Arizona">
         Pinetop-Lakeside, Arizona
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Hollingsworth was attacked by a 250lb (113.4kg) black bear while walking her dog at a country club. Nearly a month later and after eleven surgeries, she died from a massive brain hemorrhage, which doctors believe was a result of the attack. The bear was tracked, shot and killed.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters26july2011_11-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-reuters26july2011-11">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          11
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Bernice Adolph, 72, female
       </td>
       <td>
        June 2011
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        near
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lillooet,_British_Columbia" title="Lillooet, British Columbia">
         Lillooet, British Columbia
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Adolph's remains were found by police dogs after she was reported missing. She was an
        <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_elder" title="American Indian elder">
         elder
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Xaxli%27p_First_Nation" title="Xaxli'p First Nation">
         Xaxli'p First Nation
        </a>
        . There was evidence that bears fed on Adolph's remains, and tried to enter her house. An autopsy confirmed that she died from a bear attack. Five bears suspected of being involved were killed by conservation officers, and DNA tests confirmed that one of the dead bears killed Adolph.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc14july2011_12-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cbc14july2011-12">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          12
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Brent Kandra, 24, male
       </td>
       <td>
        August 19, 2010
       </td>
       <td>
        Captive
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_Station,_Ohio" title="Columbia Station, Ohio">
         Columbia Station, Ohio
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Kandra was a bear caretaker on property that kept exotic pets. The bear was out of its cage for feeding. Prior to the attack, the property's owner had his license to exhibit animals revoked, but was still allowed to keep the animals on his property.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nprAug202010_13-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nprAug202010-13">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          13
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brown_bear">
       Brown bear
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Brown bear">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Name, age, gender
       </th>
       <th style="width:100px;">
        Date
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Type
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Location
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Description

      <tr>
       <td>
        Claudia Huber, 42, female
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002014-10-18-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         October 18, 2014
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        near
        <a href="/wiki/Johnsons_Crossing" title="Johnsons Crossing">
         Johnsons Crossing
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">
         Yukon
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Huber's husband encountered a bear outside of his house. When he went inside to retrieve a rifle, the bear came into the house through a window. After Huber and her spouse fled the house, the bear pursued and killed Huber. Huber's husband shot the bear dead.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huber_cbc_14-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-huber_cbc-14">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          14
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Ken Novotny, 53, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002014-09-17-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         September 17, 2014
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        near
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Wells,_Northwest_Territories" title="Norman Wells, Northwest Territories">
         Norman Wells, Northwest Territories
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        While on a hunting trip near Normal Wells, Novotny was charged and struck by a bear. Friends reported Novotny had just killed a moose and was prepping his prize when the bear "came out of nowhere." He died on the scene. Authorities later found and killed the bear responsible for his death.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-novotny_15-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-novotny-15">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          15
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Adam Thomas Stewart, 31, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002014-09-01-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         September 2014
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Bridger-Teton_National_Forest" title="Bridger-Teton National Forest">
         Bridger-Teton National Forest
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">
         Wyoming
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Stewart was conducting research alone in the Bridger-Teton National Forest in northwest
        <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">
         Wyoming
        </a>
        . After he failed to return, a search found his body.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moen_16-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-moen-16">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          16
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        The coroner suspects it was a grizzly bear, but the species hasn't officially been determined.
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Rick Cross, 54, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002014-09-07-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         September 7, 2014
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Kananaskis_Country" title="Kananaskis Country">
         Kananaskis Country
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">
         Alberta
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Cross, a hunter, was killed by a mother bear when he accidentally got between her and her cubs. His body was found near his backpack. RCMP said it appeared he wandered into the area where the mother and cub were feeding on a dead deer.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc_cross_17-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cbc_cross-17">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          17
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Richard White, 49, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002012-08-24-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         August 24, 2012
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denali_National_Park" title="Denali National Park">
         Denali National Park
        </a>
        , Alaska
       </td>
       <td>
        White was backpacking alone along the
        <a href="/wiki/Toklat_River" title="Toklat River">
         Toklat River
        </a>
        . After hikers found an abandoned backpack and torn clothing, rangers investigated and found a male grizzly bear sitting on White's remains. The bear was shot and killed by an
        <a href="/wiki/Alaska_State_Troopers" title="Alaska State Troopers">
         Alaska State Trooper
        </a>
        . A
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necropsy" title="Necropsy">
         necropsy
        </a>
        of the bear and photographs recovered from White's camera confirmed the attack.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fairbanks82512_18-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-fairbanks82512-18">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          18
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <p>
         The photographs in White's camera showed that he was taking photos of the bear in a span of eight minutes from 50 yards (46m) to 100 yards (91m).
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apDenaliAttack_19-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-apDenaliAttack-19">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           19
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         It was the first fatal bear attack recorded in Denali National Park.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fairbanks82512_18-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-fairbanks82512-18">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           18
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Tomas Puerta, 54, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002012-10-01-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         October 2012
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Chichagof_Island" title="Chichagof Island">
         Chichagof Island
        </a>
        , Alaska
       </td>
       <td>
        After passers-by spotted an unattended
        <a href="/wiki/Skiff" title="Skiff">
         skiff
        </a>
        , they investigated and encountered a grizzly bear sow and two cubs. Alaska State troopers and
        <a href="/wiki/Sitka,_Alaska" title="Sitka, Alaska">
         Sitka
        </a>
        Mountain rescue personnel then found evidence of a campsite and fire on the beach. There was evidence of a struggle, and upon following a trail of disturbed vegetation, they found Puerta's body, cached and partially eaten.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mud_20-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-mud-20">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          20
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        John Wallace, 59, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002011-08-24-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         August 24, 2011
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">
         Yellowstone National Park
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wallace's remains were found by hikers on the Mary Mountain Trail, northeast of
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Faithful" title="Old Faithful">
         Old Faithful
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_shaylor_21-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-abc_shaylor-21">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          21
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        Wallace was hiking alone.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap_brown_2011_22-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ap_brown_2011-22">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          22
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        An autopsy showed that Wallace died from a bear attack.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap_brown_2011_22-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-ap_brown_2011-22">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          22
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        According to a report released by Yellowstone rangers, park officials attempted to give Wallace a lecture about bear safety, but he was not interested, calling himself a "grizzly bear expert".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wallace_yellowstone_23-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-wallace_yellowstone-23">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          23
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <p>
         DNA evidence later determined that the sow that killed Brian Matayoshi the month before was in the vicinity of Wallace's corpse, though it was not proved that this bear killed Wallace. The bear was killed by park officials.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_yellowstone_24-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-slate_yellowstone-24">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           24
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         Evidence showed that Wallace was attacked after sitting down on a log to eat a snack and the attack was predatory, rather than defensive.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_yellowstone_24-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-slate_yellowstone-24">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           24
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlive_yellowstone_25-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-mlive_yellowstone-25">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           25
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Brian Matayoshi, 57, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002011-07-06-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         July 6, 2011
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">
         Yellowstone National Park
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Matayoshi and his wife were hiking the Wapiti Lake Trail, and came upon a mother grizzly bear in an open
        <a href="/wiki/Meadow" title="Meadow">
         meadow
        </a>
        . The couple began to walk away, and the bear charged. After attempting to run away, Matayoshi was fatally bitten and clawed. Matayoshi's wife hid behind a tree, was lifted from the ground by the bear, and dropped. She played dead, and the bear left the area. She was not injured.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnjuly72011_26-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cnnjuly72011-26">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          26
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps_release_yellowstonejuly2011_27-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nps_release_yellowstonejuly2011-27">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          27
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <p>
         An initial investigation by the
         <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">
          National Park Service
         </a>
         found the bear's actions were defensive against a perceived threat to her cubs. Since the attack was not predatory and the bear had no known violent history towards humans, no immediate action was taken towards the bear, although it was later euthanized after it was found to be at the site of another fatal attack later that summer.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_yellowstone_24-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-slate_yellowstone-24">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           24
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnjuly72011_26-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-cnnjuly72011-26">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           26
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps_release_yellowstonejuly2011_27-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-nps_release_yellowstonejuly2011-27">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           27
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         A later investigation determined that the couple running from the bear was a mistake, and the fatal attack was a "one in 3 million occurrence".
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lat_20Sept_28-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-lat_20Sept-28">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           28
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Kevin Kammer, 48, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002010-07-28-0000

        <span style="white-space:nowrap">

Two other campers in separate campsites were also attacked: a teenager was bitten in the leg, and a woman was bitten in the arm and leg. The bear was caught in a trap set at the campground using pieces of a
        <a href="/wiki/Culvert" title="Culvert">
         culvert
        </a>
        and Kammer's tent.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap_29July2010_29-0">

          <span>
           [
          </span>
          29
          <span>

        <a href="/wiki/ZooMontana" title="ZooMontana">
         ZooMontana
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc30July2010_30-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-abc30July2010-30">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          30
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>

        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc30July2010_30-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-abc30July2010-30">

          </span>

          <span>

      <tr>
       <td>
        Erwin Frank Evert, 70, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002010-06-17-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         June 17, 2010
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Shoshone_National_Forest" title="Shoshone National Forest">
         Shoshone National Forest, Wyoming
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Evert, a field botanist, was mauled by a grizzly bear while hiking in the Kitty Creek Drainage area of the Shoshone National Forest, just east of
        <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">
         Yellowstone National Park
        </a>
        . The bear was trapped and tranquilized earlier in the day by a Grizzly Bear research team. Two days after the attack, the bear was shot and killed from a helicopter by wildlife officials.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billings_2010_31-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-billings_2010-31">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          31
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <p>
         Initially it was reported that Evert ignored posted warnings to avoid the area due to the potential danger involved with the bear research.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billings_2010_31-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-billings_2010-31">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           31
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         However, the sheriff's deputy who recovered the body and members of Evert's family stated that the warning signs were no longer present.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap_gruver_32-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-ap_gruver-32">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           32
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         A report released the following month confirmed that the warning signs were removed, though it also asserted that Evert knew there was a bear research study being conducted in the area.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billings_gazette29June2010_33-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-billings_gazette29June2010-33">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           33
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         Evert's wife filed a wrongful death lawsuit against the federal government, which was dismissed by district court judge
         <a href="/wiki/Nancy_D._Freudenthal" title="Nancy D. Freudenthal">
          Nancy D. Freudenthal
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawsuit_powell_34-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-lawsuit_powell-34">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           34
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cody_enterprise_35-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-cody_enterprise-35">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           35
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </td>

       <td>
        Donna Munson, 74, female
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000002009-08-06-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         August 6, 2009
        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Ouray,_Colorado" title="Ouray, Colorado">
         Ouray, Colorado
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Munson had been feeding bears for a decade, and was repeatedly warned by wildlife officials. After a bear was injured in a fight with an older and bigger bear, Munson left food out to help the injured bear. The older bear came back to Munson's property, forced its way past a wire fence, and mauled Munson. Later, wildlife officials killed two bears on Munson's property. One of the bears had a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necropsy" title="Necropsy">
         necropsy
        </a>
        which revealed evidence that it consumed Munson.

       <td>
        Raymond Kitchen, 56, male
        <br/>
        Patti McConnell, 37, female
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">

        <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">
         British Columbia
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        McConnell died from injuries while defending herself and her son from a black bear attack on a boardwalk to the hot springs. Kitchen heard the attack in progress, and was killed while attempting to rescue. McConnell's son and a 20-year-old man were also injured. The bear was shot while standing over the victims.

The grizzly bear that attacked and her three cubs were soon found and killed.

       </a>

     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:200px;">

       </th>

       <td>
        Lyndom Hooper, 51, male
       </td>
       <td>
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none;speak:none">
         000000001959-09-06-0000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">

        </span>
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        near
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadomin,_Alberta" title="Cadomin, Alberta">
         Cadomin, Alberta
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Hooper was fishing alone when attacked 20 miles (32km) from Cadomin, Alberta. His mutilated body was found in a stream.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sask1959_176-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-sask1959-176">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          176
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        Three days later, a forest ranger shot a bear .5 miles (0.80km) from where Hooper's body was recovered.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherald17Sep1959_177-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cherald17Sep1959-177">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          177
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        It was later discovered that the bear's stomach contained human hair.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaderPost7Oct1959_178-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-leaderPost7Oct1959-178">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          178
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>

       </td>

       </td>

        On his walk home from school, Taylor stopped to feed an apple to a bear
        <a href="/wiki/Tether" title="Tether">
         tethered
        </a>
        in front of an inn. The bear mauled Taylor and crushed him against a wire cage. Motorists stopped and used sticks and stones to try to separate the bear from Taylor.
        <p>
         Eventually, a man operating a nearby
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roadside_stand" title="Roadside stand">
          roadside stand
         </a>
         came and shot and killed the bear. An examination revealed that the bear hadn't eaten in two days. The Inn had two bears that were trapped five years previously in the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adirondacks" title="Adirondacks">
          Adirondacks
         </a>
         , and were frequently fed by passers-by. Both bears were killed.

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brown_bear_12">
       Brown bear
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Brown bear">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:200px;">
        Name, age, gender
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Date
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Type
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:150px;">
        Location
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Description
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Phillip Henry Vetter, 37, male
       </td>
       <td>
        Sept. 2 1892
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        Near
        <a href="/wiki/Greybull_River" title="Greybull River">
         Greybull River
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">
         Wyoming
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Vetter, a
        <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">
         buffalo
        </a>
        hunter, had stated to a fellow hunter a week before, that "he would be going out for some bear." A week later, another hunter seeking shelter from rain, investigated his cabin, which had been ransacked by a grizzly bear, and found Vetter dead in the cabin. A newspaper found, had text written in blood by Vetter before his death, describing his battle with a grizzly bear, and ending with the words "I'm dying." The hunter, and another man, searched for signs of the bear, and found Vetter's hat, two empty rifle shells, and his rifle with a shell jammed in its chamber. Vetter was buried in
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Trail_Town" title="Old Trail Town">
         Old Trail Town
        </a>
        , Cody, Wyoming.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
         <a href="#cite_note-216">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          216
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>

        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt1883_217-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nyt1883-217">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          217
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        John Dennison, 82, male
       </td>
       <td>
        June 1881
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        Happy Lake Isle, Ontario
       </td>
       <td>
        This was Ontario's first recorded fatal black bear attack. Dennison was inspecting his bear traps on Happy Lake Isle, in what is now
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algonquin_Park" title="Algonquin Park">
         Algonquin Park
        </a>
        . After discovering a bear caught in the trap a struggle between the two ensued, ending in both their deaths.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
         <a href="#cite_note-218">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          218
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brown_bear_13">
       Brown bear
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Brown bear">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:200px;">
        Name, age, gender
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Date
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Type
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:150px;">
        Location
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Description
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        "Old Ike,"?, male
       </td>
       <td>
        June 1886
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Salmon_River_(Idaho)" title="Salmon River (Idaho)">
         Salmon River (Idaho)
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        One of the most successful bear hunters, he killed over 100 grizzlies during the 1800s. In June 1886, he wounded a grizzly bear near the headwaters of the Salmon River, but not enough to cripple the animal. After pursuing it into a dense stand of trees, he was attacked by the bear and mauled, with the bear biting into his chest. Within a few minutes his entire chest had been crushed by the one bite. The bear was driven off by his companions.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bear_alm_219-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-bear_alm-219">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          219
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Richard Wilson,?, male
       </td>
       <td>
        July 1885
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Sedona,_Arizona" title="Sedona, Arizona">
         Sedona, Arizona
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        A bear hunter, Wilson was hunting grizzly bears and shot one near Oak Creek, Arizona. He followed the bear into a thicket and was attacked. He tried to escape by climbing a tree, but the bear pulled him down and killed him.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bear_alm_219-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-bear_alm-219">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          219
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Hyrum Naegle,?, male
       </td>
       <td>
        Circa 1885
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Colonia_Pacheco&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Colonia Pacheco (page does not exist)">
         Colonia Pacheco
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_(state)" title="Chihuahua (state)">
         Chihuahua, Mexico
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Hyrum and his brother shot and wounded a grizzly bear which they pursued, thinking it would soon expire. While brother George was repairing his gun, Hyrum went to shoot the bear but was mauled and killed when the bear crushed his skull. The bear was shot and killed by George's repaired rifle.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bear_alm_219-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-bear_alm-219">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          219
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brown_bear_16">
       Brown bear
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Brown bear">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:200px;">
        Name, age, gender
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Date
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">

       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:150px;">

      <tr>
       <td>
        Andy Sublette, 46, male
       </td>
       <td>
        December 19, 1854
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Santa_Monica,_California" title="Santa Monica, California">
         Santa Monica, California
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        An experienced bear hunter who hunted and killed many bears, Sublette shot and wounded a bear after being separated from his hunting party near present-day Santa Monica in 1854. He was then mauled but stabbed the bear to death with his knife and with the help of his dog. His dog survived, but Sublette died seven days later due to his injuries.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bear_alm_219-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-bear_alm-219">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          219
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Issac Slover, 81, male
       </td>
       <td>
        October 14, 1854
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Mount_San_Antonio" title="Mount San Antonio">
         Mount San Antonio
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        A trapper and hunter who hunted grizzly bears even in his older ages, Slover shot and wounded one on Mount San Antonio near his cabin. The large wounded bear crawled into the brush, and Slover reloaded and followed, where he was attacked, torn to pieces, and died.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bear_alm_219-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-bear_alm-219">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          219
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Jim Boggs,?, male
       </td>
       <td>
        Circa 1850
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_River,_California" title="Russian River, California">
         Russian River, California
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        While hunting grizzly bears, he blundered into the den of a mother with cubs. He was attacked and killed.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bear_alm_219-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-bear_alm-219">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          219
          <span>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1830s">
       1830s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: 1830s">

       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brown_bear_17">
       Brown bear
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_fatal_bear_attacks_in_North_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Brown bear">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable sortable">
      <tr>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:200px;">
        Name, age, gender
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:100px;">
        Date
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Type
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable" style="width:150px;">
        Location
       </th>
       <th class="unsortable">
        Description
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Peter Lebec,?, male
       </td>
       <td>
        October 17, 1837
       </td>
       <td>
        Wild
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Tejon,_California" title="Fort Tejon, California">
         Fort Tejon, California
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Lebec was out hunting grizzlies. He dispatched one with a well-placed shot and, assuming it to be dead, approached it. However, the bear rallied and lunged, throwing him to the ground and breaking his neck. He was killed instantly, but the bear was later killed by Native American hunters.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
         <a href="#cite_note-225">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          225
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
